<p><b>Chester Alan Arthur</b> (October&#160;5, 1829&#160;– November&#160;18, 1886) was an American attorney and politician who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">21st</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> (1881–1885); he succeeded <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> upon the latter's <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_James_A._Garfield" title="Assassination of James A. Garfield">assassination</a>. At the outset, Arthur struggled to overcome a slightly negative reputation, which stemmed from his early career in politics as part of <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">New York's</a> <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_machine" title="Political machine">political machine</a>. He succeeded by embracing the cause of <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_civil_service" title="United States federal civil service">civil service</a> reform. His advocacy for, and subsequent enforcement of, the <a href="/wiki/Pendleton_Civil_Service_Reform_Act" title="Pendleton Civil Service Reform Act">Pendleton Civil Service Reform Act</a> was the centerpiece of his administration.</p>
<p>Arthur was born in <a href="/wiki/Fairfield,_Vermont" title="Fairfield, Vermont">Fairfield, Vermont</a>, grew up in <a href="/wiki/Upstate_New_York" title="Upstate New York">upstate New York</a>, and practiced law in New York City. He served as <a href="/wiki/Quartermaster_general" title="Quartermaster general">quartermaster general</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Army_National_Guard" title="New York Army National Guard">New York Militia</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. Following the war, he devoted more time to Republican politics and quickly rose in the political machine run by New York Senator <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Conkling" title="Roscoe Conkling">Roscoe Conkling</a>. Appointed by President <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a> to the lucrative and politically powerful post of <a href="/wiki/Collector_of_the_Port_of_New_York" title="Collector of the Port of New York">Collector of the Port of New York</a> in 1871, Arthur was an important supporter of Conkling and the <a href="/wiki/Stalwart_(politics)" title="Stalwart (politics)">Stalwart</a> faction of the Republican Party. In 1878 the new president, <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">Rutherford B. Hayes</a>, fired Arthur as part of a plan to reform the federal <a href="/wiki/Patronage_system" title="Patronage system" class="mw-redirect">patronage system</a> in New York. When Garfield won the Republican nomination for <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">president</a> in 1880, Arthur, an eastern Stalwart, was nominated for <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">vice president</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ticket_balance" title="Ticket balance">balance the ticket</a>.</p>
<p>After just half a year as vice president, Arthur found himself in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">executive mansion</a> due to the assassination of his predecessor. To the surprise of reformers, Arthur took up the cause of reform, though it had once led to his expulsion from office. He signed the Pendleton Act into law and strongly enforced its provisions. He gained praise for his veto of a <a href="/wiki/Rivers_and_Harbors_Act" title="Rivers and Harbors Act">Rivers and Harbors Act</a> that would have appropriated federal funds in a manner he thought excessive. He presided over the rebirth of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> but was criticized for failing to alleviate the federal budget surplus, which had been accumulating since the end of the Civil War.</p>
<p>Suffering from poor health, Arthur made only a limited effort to secure the Republican Party's nomination in 1884; he retired at the close of his term. Journalist <a href="/wiki/Alexander_McClure" title="Alexander McClure">Alexander McClure</a> later wrote, "No man ever entered the Presidency so profoundly and widely distrusted as Chester Alan Arthur, and no one ever retired&#160;... more generally respected, alike by political friend and foe."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975420_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975420-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> Although his failing health and political temperament combined to make his administration less active than a modern presidency, he earned praise among contemporaries for his solid performance in office. The <i>New York World</i> summed up Arthur's presidency at his death in 1886: "No duty was neglected in his administration, and no adventurous project alarmed the nation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975423_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975423-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a> wrote of him, "[I]t would be hard indeed to better President Arthur's administration."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman95_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman95-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> Over the 20th and 21st centuries, however, Arthur's reputation mostly faded among the public. Although some have praised his economic policies, present-day historians and scholars list him among the <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">bottom half</a> when ranking the American presidents.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Election_of_1880"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Election of 1880</span></a></li>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_family">Birth and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Birth and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chester Alan Arthur was born October&#160;5, 1829, in <a href="/wiki/Fairfield,_Vermont" title="Fairfield, Vermont">Fairfield, Vermont</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> His father, William Arthur, was born just outside the village of <a href="/wiki/Cullybackey" title="Cullybackey">Cullybackey</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">County Antrim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>). William Arthur emigrated to <a href="/wiki/Dunham,_Quebec" title="Dunham, Quebec">Dunham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lower_Canada" title="Lower Canada">Lower Canada</a>, (in present-day <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>) in 1818 or 1819, after graduating from <a href="/wiki/Royal_Belfast_Academical_Institution" title="Royal Belfast Academical Institution">Belfast College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur's mother, Malvina Stone, was born in <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, the daughter of George Washington Stone and Judith Stevens. Malvina's family was primarily of English descent, and her grandfather, Uriah Stone, fought in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur's mother met his father while William Arthur was teaching at a school in Dunham, just over the border from her native Vermont. The two married in Dunham on April 12, 1821, soon after meeting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> After their first child, Regina, was born, the Arthurs moved to Vermont. They quickly moved from <a href="/wiki/Burlington,_Vermont" title="Burlington, Vermont">Burlington</a> to <a href="/wiki/Jericho,_Vermont" title="Jericho, Vermont">Jericho</a>, and finally to <a href="/wiki/Waterville,_Vermont" title="Waterville, Vermont">Waterville</a>, as William received positions teaching at different schools.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> William Arthur also spent a brief time working to join the legal profession. While still in Waterville, William departed from his legal studies, as well as his <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterian</a> upbringing, to join the <a href="/wiki/Free_Will_Baptist" title="Free Will Baptist">Free Will Baptists</a>, spending the rest of his life as a minister in that sect.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19754Howe4-7"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> William became an outspoken abolitionist, which often made him unpopular with members of his congregations and contributed to the family's frequent moves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe7Reeves19756_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe7Reeves19756-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1828, the family moved again, to Fairfield, where Chester Alan Arthur was born the following year; he was the fifth of nine children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19755_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19755-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe5.2C25.2C28.2C29_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe5.2C25.2C28.2C29-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> He was named "Chester" after Chester Abell, the physician and family friend who assisted in his birth, and "Alan" for his paternal grandfather.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup> The family remained in Fairfield until 1832, when William's profession took them on the road again, to several towns in Vermont and upstate New York. The family finally settled in the <a href="/wiki/Schenectady,_New_York" title="Schenectady, New York">Schenectady, New York</a>, area.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19757.E2.80.938_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19757.E2.80.938-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The family's frequent moves later spawned accusations that Chester Arthur was not a native-born citizen of the United States. When Arthur was <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1880" title="United States presidential election, 1880">nominated for vice president in 1880</a>, a New York attorney and political opponent, Arthur P. Hinman, initially speculated that Arthur was born in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and did not come to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> until he was fourteen years old. Had that been true, opponents might have argued that Arthur was constitutionally ineligible for the vice presidency under the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Natural-born-citizen_clause_of_the_U.S._Constitution" title="Natural-born-citizen clause of the U.S. Constitution" class="mw-redirect">natural-born citizen clause</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell53.E2.80.9354_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell53.E2.80.9354-12"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>c<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975202.E2.80.93203_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975202.E2.80.93203-14"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> When Hinman's original story did not take root, he spread a new rumor that Arthur was born in Canada. This claim, too, failed to gain credence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975202.E2.80.93203_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975202.E2.80.93203-14"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Arthur spent some of his childhood years living in the New York towns of <a href="/wiki/York,_New_York" title="York, New York">York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perry,_New_York" title="Perry, New York">Perry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_(town),_New_York" title="Greenwich (town), New York">Greenwich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schenectady,_New_York" title="Schenectady, New York">Schenectady</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hoosick,_New_York" title="Hoosick, New York">Hoosick</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> One of his first teachers said Arthur was a boy "frank and open in manners and genial in disposition."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe7_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe7-16"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> During his time at school, he gained his first political inclinations and supported the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a>. He joined other young Whigs in support of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, even participating in a brawl against students who supported <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19757.E2.80.938_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19757.E2.80.938-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur also supported the <a href="/wiki/Fenian_Brotherhood" title="Fenian Brotherhood">Fenian Brotherhood</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">Irish republican</a> organization founded in America; he showed this support by wearing a green coat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19759_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19759-17"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur enrolled at <a href="/wiki/Union_College" title="Union College">Union College</a>, in Schenectady, New York, in 1845, where he studied the traditional <a href="/wiki/Classical_education_movement" title="Classical education movement">classical curriculum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19757.E2.80.938_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19757.E2.80.938-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> As a <a href="/wiki/Senior_(education)#Higher_education" title="Senior (education)">senior</a>, he was president of the debate society and was elected to <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa" title="Phi Beta Kappa" class="mw-redirect">Phi Beta Kappa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19759_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19759-17"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> During his winter breaks, Arthur served as a teacher at a school in <a href="/wiki/Schaghticoke_(town),_New_York" title="Schaghticoke (town), New York">Schaghticoke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves19759_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves19759-17"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After graduating, Arthur returned to Schaghticoke and became a full-time teacher, but soon began to pursue an education in the law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197510_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197510-18"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> While studying law, he continued teaching, moving closer to home by taking a job at a school in <a href="/wiki/North_Pownal,_Vermont" title="North Pownal, Vermont" class="mw-redirect">North Pownal, Vermont</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197510_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197510-18"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Coincidentally, future president James A. Garfield taught penmanship at the same school three years later, but the two did not cross paths during their teaching careers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197511_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197511-19"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1852, Arthur moved again, to <a href="/wiki/Cohoes,_New_York" title="Cohoes, New York">Cohoes, New York</a>, to become the principal of a school at which his sister, Malvina, was a teacher.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197511_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197511-19"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1853, after studying at <a href="/wiki/State_and_National_Law_School" title="State and National Law School">State and National Law School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ballston_Spa,_New_York" title="Ballston Spa, New York">Ballston Spa, New York</a>, and then saving enough money to relocate, Arthur moved to New York City to <a href="/wiki/Reading_law" title="Reading law">read law</a> at the law office of <a href="/wiki/Erastus_D._Culver" title="Erastus D. Culver">Erastus D. Culver</a>, an abolitionist lawyer and family friend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell12_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell12-20"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> When Arthur was admitted to the <a href="/wiki/Bar_(law)" title="Bar (law)">bar</a> in 1854, he joined Culver's firm, which was subsequently renamed Culver, Parker, and Arthur.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197514_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197514-21"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career">Early career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_lawyer">New York lawyer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: New York lawyer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chester_A_Arthur_1859.png" class="image"><img alt="Black-and-white photograph of a young man with a mustache" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Chester_A_Arthur_1859.png/170px-Chester_A_Arthur_1859.png" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Chester_A_Arthur_1859.png/255px-Chester_A_Arthur_1859.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Chester_A_Arthur_1859.png 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="390" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chester_A_Arthur_1859.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arthur as a young lawyer</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Arthur joined the firm, Culver and New York attorney <a href="/wiki/John_Jay_(lawyer)" title="John Jay (lawyer)">John Jay</a> (the grandson of the Founding Father of the <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">same name</a>) were pursuing a <i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a></i> action against Jonathan Lemmon, a Virginia slaveholder who was passing through New York with his eight slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197514.E2.80.9315_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197514.E2.80.9315-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Lemmon_v._New_York" title="Lemmon v. New York">Lemmon v. New York</a></i>, Culver argued that, as New York law did not permit slavery, any slave arriving in New York was automatically freed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197514.E2.80.9315_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197514.E2.80.9315-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The argument was successful, and after several appeals was upheld by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Court_of_Appeals" title="New York Court of Appeals">New York Court of Appeals</a> in 1860.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197514.E2.80.9315_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197514.E2.80.9315-22"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Campaign biographers would later give Arthur much of the credit for the victory; in fact his role was minor, although he was certainly an active participant in the case.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197516_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197516-23"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> In another civil rights case in 1854, Arthur was the lead attorney representing <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Jennings_Graham" title="Elizabeth Jennings Graham">Elizabeth Jennings Graham</a> after she was denied a seat on a streetcar because she was black.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197516_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197516-23"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> He won the case, and the verdict led to the <a href="/wiki/Desegregation" title="Desegregation">desegregation</a> of the New York City streetcar lines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197516_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197516-23"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1856, Arthur courted <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Lewis_Herndon_Arthur" title="Ellen Lewis Herndon Arthur">Ellen Herndon</a>, the daughter of <a href="/wiki/William_Lewis_Herndon" title="William Lewis Herndon">William Lewis Herndon</a>, a Virginia naval officer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197519.E2.80.9320_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197519.E2.80.9320-24"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> The two were soon engaged to be married.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell14_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell14-25"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> Later that year, he started a new law partnership with a friend, Henry D. Gardiner, and traveled with him to Kansas to consider purchasing land and setting up a law practice there.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197516_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197516-23"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> At that time, the state was the scene of a <a href="/wiki/Bleeding_Kansas" title="Bleeding Kansas">brutal struggle</a> between pro-slavery and anti-slavery forces, and Arthur lined up firmly with the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197517.E2.80.9318_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197517.E2.80.9318-26"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> The rough frontier life did not agree with the genteel New Yorkers; after three or four months the two young lawyers returned to New York City, where Arthur comforted his fiancée after her father was lost at sea in the wreck of the <a href="/wiki/SS_Central_America" title="SS Central America">SS <i>Central America</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197517.E2.80.9318_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197517.E2.80.9318-26"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1859, they were married at <a href="/wiki/Calvary_Church_(Manhattan)" title="Calvary Church (Manhattan)">Calvary Episcopal Church</a> in Manhattan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197521_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197521-27"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> After his marriage, Arthur devoted his efforts to building his law practice, but also found time to engage in Republican party politics. He also indulged his military interest by becoming <a href="/wiki/Judge_Advocate_General%27s_Corps,_United_States_Army" title="Judge Advocate General's Corps, United States Army">Judge Advocate General</a> for the Second Brigade of the New York Militia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe18.E2.80.9319_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe18.E2.80.9319-28"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War">Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Arthur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Black-and-white photograph of a woman with dark hair" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Ellen_Arthur.jpg/170px-Ellen_Arthur.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Ellen_Arthur.jpg/255px-Ellen_Arthur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Ellen_Arthur.jpg/340px-Ellen_Arthur.jpg 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="585" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellen_Arthur.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arthur married <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Lewis_Herndon_Arthur" title="Ellen Lewis Herndon Arthur">Ellen Herndon</a> in 1859.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1860, Arthur was appointed to the military staff of Governor <a href="/wiki/Edwin_D._Morgan" title="Edwin D. Morgan">Edwin D. Morgan</a> as engineer-in-chief.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe18.E2.80.9319_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe18.E2.80.9319-28"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> The office was a patronage appointment of minor importance until the outbreak of the Civil War in April 1861, when New York and the other northern states were faced with raising and equipping armies of a size never before seen in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe20.E2.80.9321Reeves197522.E2.80.9323_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe20.E2.80.9321Reeves197522.E2.80.9323-29"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur was given the rank of <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general_(United_States)" title="Brigadier general (United States)">brigadier general</a> and assigned to the <a href="/wiki/Quartermaster" title="Quartermaster">quartermaster</a> department.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe20.E2.80.9321Reeves197522.E2.80.9323_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe20.E2.80.9321Reeves197522.E2.80.9323-29"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> He was so efficient at housing and outfitting the troops that poured into New York City that he was promoted within the state militia to inspector general in February 1862, and then to quartermaster general that July.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197524.E2.80.9325_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197524.E2.80.9325-30"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> He had an opportunity to serve at the front when the <a href="/wiki/9th_New_York_Volunteer_Infantry_Regiment" title="9th New York Volunteer Infantry Regiment">9th New York Volunteer Infantry Regiment</a> elected him <a href="/wiki/Colonel" title="Colonel">colonel</a> early in the war, but at Governor Morgan's request, he turned it down to remain at his post in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe25_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe25-31"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> He also turned down command of four New York City regiments organized as the Metropolitan Brigade, again at Morgan's request.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe25_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe25-31"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> The closest Arthur came to the front was when he traveled south to inspect New York troops near <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia" title="Fredericksburg, Virginia">Fredericksburg</a>, Virginia, in May 1862, shortly after forces under Major General <a href="/wiki/Irwin_McDowell" title="Irwin McDowell" class="mw-redirect">Irwin McDowell</a> seized the town during the <a href="/wiki/Peninsula_Campaign" title="Peninsula Campaign">Peninsula Campaign</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe26.E2.80.9327Reeves197528.E2.80.9329_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe26.E2.80.9327Reeves197528.E2.80.9329-32"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> That summer, he and other representatives of northern governors met with Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/William_H._Seward" title="William H. Seward">William H. Seward</a> in New York to coordinate the raising of additional troops, and spent the next few months enlisting New York's quota of 120,000 men.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe26.E2.80.9327Reeves197528.E2.80.9329_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe26.E2.80.9327Reeves197528.E2.80.9329-32"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur received plaudits for his work, but his post was a political appointment, and he was relieved of his militia duties in January 1863 when Governor <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Seymour" title="Horatio Seymour">Horatio Seymour</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a>, took office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197530_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197530-33"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Arthur later joined the <a href="/wiki/Military_Order_of_the_Loyal_Legion_of_the_United_States" title="Military Order of the Loyal Legion of the United States">Military Order of the Loyal Legion of the United States</a> (MOLLUS) as a Third Class Companion, the honorary membership category for militia officers and civilians who made significant contributions to the war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic" title="Grand Army of the Republic">Grand Army of the Republic</a> posts in <a href="/wiki/Ogdensburg,_Wisconsin" title="Ogdensburg, Wisconsin">Ogdensburg, Wisconsin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medford,_Oregon" title="Medford, Oregon">Medford, Oregon</a> were named in his honor.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Arthur returned to his law practice in 1863 and, with the help of additional contacts made in the military, he and the firm of Arthur &amp; Gardiner flourished.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe30.E2.80.9331Reeves197533.E2.80.9334_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe30.E2.80.9331Reeves197533.E2.80.9334-37"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Even as his professional life improved, however, Arthur and his wife experienced a personal tragedy as their only child, William, died suddenly that year at the age of three.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe29.E2.80.9330Reeves197534.E2.80.9335_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe29.E2.80.9330Reeves197534.E2.80.9335-38"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> The couple took their son's death hard, and when they had another son, Chester Alan Jr., in 1864, they lavished attention on him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197535_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197535-39"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> They also had a daughter, Ellen, in 1871. Both children survived to adulthood.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197584_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197584-40"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Arthur's political prospects improved along with his law practice when his patron, ex-Governor Morgan, was elected to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197537_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197537-41"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> He was hired by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Murphy_(Collector)" title="Thomas Murphy (Collector)">Thomas Murphy</a>, a Republican politician, but also a friend of <a href="/wiki/William_M._Tweed" title="William M. Tweed">William M. Tweed</a>, the boss of the <a href="/wiki/Tammany_Hall" title="Tammany Hall">Tammany Hall</a> Democratic organization. Murphy was also a <a href="/wiki/Hatmaking" title="Hatmaking">hatter</a> who sold goods to the Union Army, and Arthur represented him in Washington. The two became associates within New York Republican party circles, eventually rising in the ranks of the conservative branch of the party dominated by <a href="/wiki/Thurlow_Weed" title="Thurlow Weed">Thurlow Weed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197537_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197537-41"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1864" title="United States presidential election, 1864">presidential election of 1864</a>, Arthur and Murphy raised funds from Republicans in New York and attended <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Second_inauguration_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Second inauguration of Abraham Lincoln">inauguration in 1865</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197538_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197538-42"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_politician">New York politician</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: New York politician">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conkling.27s_machine">Conkling's machine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Conkling's machine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NYCustomHouseMerchantsExchange.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A columned building with a domed roof" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/NYCustomHouseMerchantsExchange.jpg/250px-NYCustomHouseMerchantsExchange.jpg" width="250" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/NYCustomHouseMerchantsExchange.jpg/375px-NYCustomHouseMerchantsExchange.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/NYCustomHouseMerchantsExchange.jpg/500px-NYCustomHouseMerchantsExchange.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="475" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NYCustomHouseMerchantsExchange.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/New_York_Custom_House" title="New York Custom House" class="mw-redirect">New York Custom House</a> (formerly the Merchants' Exchange building at <a href="/wiki/55_Wall_Street" title="55 Wall Street">55 Wall Street</a>) was Arthur's office for seven years.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The end of the Civil War meant new opportunities for the men in Morgan's Republican <a href="/wiki/Political_machine" title="Political machine">machine</a>, including Arthur.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell17_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell17-43"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Morgan leaned toward the conservative wing of the New York Republican party, as did the men who worked for him, including Weed, Seward (who continued in office under President <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Conkling" title="Roscoe Conkling">Roscoe Conkling</a> (an eloquent <a href="/wiki/Utica,_New_York" title="Utica, New York">Utica</a> Congressman and rising star in the party).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell17_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell17-43"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur rarely articulated his own political ideas during his time as a part of the machine; as was common at the time, loyalty and hard work on the machine's behalf was more important than actual political sympathies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197539Howe37_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197539Howe37-44"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the time, U.S. Custom Houses were managed by political appointees who served as Collector, Naval Officer and Surveyor. In 1866, Arthur unsuccessfully attempted to secure the position of Naval Officer at the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Custom_House" title="New York Custom House" class="mw-redirect">New York Custom House</a>, a lucrative job subordinate only to the Collector.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197540.E2.80.9341_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197540.E2.80.9341-45"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> He continued his law practice (now a solo practitionership after Gardiner's death) and his role in politics, becoming a member of the prestigious <a href="/wiki/Century_Association" title="Century Association">Century Club</a> in 1867.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197540.E2.80.9341_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197540.E2.80.9341-45"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Conkling, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_New_York,_1867" title="United States Senate election in New York, 1867">elected in 1867</a> to the United States Senate, noticed Arthur and facilitated his rise in the party, and Arthur became chairman of the New York City Republican executive committee in 1868.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197542.E2.80.9345_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197542.E2.80.9345-46"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> His ascent in the party hierarchy kept him busy most nights, and his wife resented his continual absence from the family home on party business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197571.E2.80.9373_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197571.E2.80.9373-47"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Conkling's machine was solidly behind General Ulysses S. Grant's candidacy for president, and Arthur raised funds for Grant's election in 1868.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197548_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197548-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> The opposing Democratic machine in New York City, known as <a href="/wiki/Tammany_Hall" title="Tammany Hall">Tammany Hall</a>, worked for Grant's opponent, former New York Governor Horatio Seymour; while Grant was victorious in the national vote, Seymour narrowly carried the state of New York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197548_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197548-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur began to devote more of his time to politics and less to law, and in 1869 he became counsel to the New York City tax commission, appointed when Republicans controlled the state legislature. He remained at the job until 1870 at a salary of $10,000 a year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197549.E2.80.9350Howe42_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197549.E2.80.9350Howe42-49"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>d<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur resigned after Democrats controlled by <a href="/wiki/William_M._Tweed" title="William M. Tweed">William M. Tweed</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tammany_Hall" title="Tammany Hall">Tammany Hall</a> won a legislative majority, which meant they could name their own appointee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe42_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe42-52"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly thereafter, President Grant gave control over the New York Custom House patronage to Conkling. Having become friendly with Murphy over their shared love of horses, Grant appointed him to Collector's position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197551.E2.80.9353Howe44.E2.80.9345_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197551.E2.80.9353Howe44.E2.80.9345-53"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Murphy's reputation as a war profiteer and his association with Tammany Hall made him unacceptable to many of his own party, but Conkling convinced the Senate to confirm him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197551.E2.80.9353Howe44.E2.80.9345_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197551.E2.80.9353Howe44.E2.80.9345-53"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> The Collector was responsible for hiring hundreds of workers to collect the tariffs due at the United States' busiest port. Typically, these jobs were dispensed to adherents of the political machine responsible for hiring the Collector. Employees were required to make political contributions (known as "assessments") back to the machine, which made the job a highly coveted political plum.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197561.E2.80.9367Schwartz182_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197561.E2.80.9367Schwartz182-54"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Murphy's unpopularity only increased as he replaced workers loyal to Senator <a href="/wiki/Reuben_Fenton" title="Reuben Fenton">Reuben Fenton</a>'s faction of the Republican party with those loyal to Conkling's.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197557.E2.80.9358_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197557.E2.80.9358-55"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Eventually, the pressure to replace Murphy grew too great, and Grant asked for his resignation in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197557.E2.80.9358_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197557.E2.80.9358-55"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant offered the position to <a href="/wiki/John_Ashley_Griswold" title="John Ashley Griswold">John Ashley Griswold</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Orton_(businessman)" title="William Orton (businessman)">William Orton</a>, each of whom declined and recommended Arthur.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoyle_.26_Swaney188_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoyle_.26_Swaney188-56"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Grant then nominated Arthur, with the <i>New York Times</i> commenting, "his name very seldom rises to the surface of metropolitan life and yet moving like a mighty undercurrent this man during the last 10 years has done more to mold the course of the Republican Party in this state than any other one man in the country."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197560Howe46.E2.80.9347_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197560Howe46.E2.80.9347-57"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Senate confirmed Arthur's appointment; as Collector he controlled nearly a thousand jobs and received compensation as great as any federal officeholder.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197561.E2.80.9367Schwartz182_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197561.E2.80.9367Schwartz182-54"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur's salary was $6,500, but senior customs employees were compensated additionally by the "moiety" system, which awarded them a percentage of the fines levied on importers who attempted to evade the tariff.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197559.2C_63.2C_85.E2.80.9386_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197559.2C_63.2C_85.E2.80.9386-58"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> In total, his income came to more than $50,000—more than the president's salary, and more than enough for him to enjoy fashionable clothes and a lavish lifestyle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197559.2C_63.2C_85.E2.80.9386_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197559.2C_63.2C_85.E2.80.9386-58"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>e<span>]</span></a></sup> Among those who dealt with the Custom House, Arthur was one of the era's more popular collectors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197568_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197568-60"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> He got along with his subordinates and, since Murphy had already filled the staff with Conkling's adherents, he had few occasions to fire anyone.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197569.E2.80.9370_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197569.E2.80.9370-61"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> He was also popular within the Republican party as he efficiently collected campaign assessments from the staff and placed party leaders' friends in jobs as positions became available.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197571.E2.80.9373_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197571.E2.80.9373-47"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur had a better reputation than Murphy, but reformers still criticized the patronage structure and the moiety system as corrupt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197561.E2.80.9367Schwartz182_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197561.E2.80.9367Schwartz182-54"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> A rising tide of reform within the party caused Arthur to rename the financial extractions from employees as "voluntary contributions" in 1872, but the concept remained, and the party reaped the benefit of controlling government jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197576.E2.80.9377_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197576.E2.80.9377-62"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> In that year, reform-minded Republicans formed the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Liberal Republican Party (United States)">Liberal Republican</a> party and voted against Grant, but he was <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1872" title="United States presidential election, 1872">re-elected</a> in spite of their opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197578.E2.80.9379_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197578.E2.80.9379-63"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, the movement for civil service reform continued to chip away at Conkling's patronage machine; in 1874 Custom House employees were found to have improperly assessed fines against an importing company as a way to increase their own incomes, and Congress reacted, repealing the moiety system and putting the staff, including Arthur, on regular salaries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197579.E2.80.9384Howe49_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197579.E2.80.9384Howe49-64"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result, his income dropped to $12,000 a year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197579.E2.80.9384Howe49_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197579.E2.80.9384Howe49-64"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clash_with_Hayes">Clash with Hayes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Clash with Hayes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Cartoon of a man kicking another man into the street" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg/250px-Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg" width="250" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg/375px-Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg/500px-Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1567" data-file-height="1269" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A cartoon depicting President <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">Rutherford B. Hayes</a> kicking Arthur out of the New York Custom House</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Arthur's four-year term expired on December&#160;10, 1875, and Conkling, then among the most powerful politicians in Washington, arranged his protégé's reappointment by President Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197587.E2.80.9389_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197587.E2.80.9389-65"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1876, Conkling was considering a run for the presidency himself, but the selection of reformer Rutherford B. Hayes by the <a href="/wiki/1876_Republican_National_Convention" title="1876 Republican National Convention">1876 Republican National Convention</a> preempted the machine boss.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves197595.E2.80.9396Karabell26.E2.80.9327_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves197595.E2.80.9396Karabell26.E2.80.9327-66"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur and the machine gathered campaign funds with their usual zeal, but Conkling limited his own campaign activities to a few speeches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975100.E2.80.93105_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975100.E2.80.93105-67"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes's opponent, New York Governor <a href="/wiki/Samuel_J._Tilden" title="Samuel J. Tilden">Samuel J. Tilden</a>, carried New York and won the popular vote nationwide, but after the resolution of several months of <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(United_States)" title="Electoral Commission (United States)">disputes over twenty electoral votes</a> (from the states of Florida, Louisiana, Oregon, and South Carolina), he lost the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975106.E2.80.93107_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975106.E2.80.93107-68"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes entered office with a pledge to reform the patronage system; in 1877, he and Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/John_Sherman" title="John Sherman">John Sherman</a> made Conkling's machine the primary target.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975100.E2.80.93105_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975100.E2.80.93105-67"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> Sherman ordered a commission led by John Jay to investigate the New York Custom House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom318.E2.80.93319_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom318.E2.80.93319-69"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Jay, with whom Arthur had collaborated in the <i>Lemmon</i> case two decades earlier, suggested that the Custom House was overstaffed with political appointments, and that 20% of the employees were expendable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Reeves1975118.E2.80.93119Howe68.E2.80.9369_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Reeves1975118.E2.80.93119Howe68.E2.80.9369-70"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Sherman was less enthusiastic about the reforms than Hayes and Jay, but he approved the commission's report and ordered Arthur to make the personnel reductions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975119.E2.80.93120_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975119.E2.80.93120-71"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur appointed a committee of Custom House workers to determine where the cuts were to be made and, after a written protest, carried them out.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975121.E2.80.93122_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975121.E2.80.93122-72"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Notwithstanding his cooperation, the Jay Commission issued a second report critical of Arthur and other Custom House employees, and subsequent reports urging a complete reorganization.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975121.E2.80.93122_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975121.E2.80.93122-72"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes further struck at the heart of the <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a> by issuing an <a href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" title="Executive order (United States)" class="mw-redirect">executive order</a> that forbade assessments and barred federal office holders from "tak[ing] part in the management of political organizations, caucuses, conventions, or election campaigns".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Reeves1975121_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Reeves1975121-73"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur and his subordinates, Naval Officer <a href="/wiki/Alonzo_B._Cornell" title="Alonzo B. Cornell">Alonzo B. Cornell</a> and Surveyor <a href="/wiki/George_H._Sharpe" title="George H. Sharpe">George H. Sharpe</a>, refused to obey the president's order; Sherman encouraged Arthur to resign, offering him appointment by Hayes to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_France" title="United States Ambassador to France">consulship in Paris</a> in exchange, but Arthur refused.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975121.E2.80.93123_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975121.E2.80.93123-74"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> In September 1877, Hayes demanded the three men's resignations, which they refused to give.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975123_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975123-75"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes then submitted the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt,_Sr." title="Theodore Roosevelt, Sr." class="mw-redirect">Theodore Roosevelt, Sr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/L._Bradford_Prince" title="L. Bradford Prince">L. Bradford Prince</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Atkins_Merritt" title="Edwin Atkins Merritt">Edwin Merritt</a> (all supporters of Conkling's rival <a href="/wiki/William_M._Evarts" title="William M. Evarts">William M. Evarts</a>) to the Senate for confirmation as their replacements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom352Reeves1975125.E2.80.93126_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom352Reeves1975125.E2.80.93126-76"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate's Commerce Committee, chaired by Conkling, unanimously rejected all the nominees; the full Senate rejected Roosevelt and Prince by a vote of 31–25, and confirmed Merritt only because Sharpe's term had expired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom353.E2.80.93355Reeves1975126.E2.80.93131_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom353.E2.80.93355Reeves1975126.E2.80.93131-77"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Arthur's job was only spared until July 1878, when Hayes took advantage of a Congressional recess to fire him and Cornell, replacing them with the <a href="/wiki/Recess_appointment" title="Recess appointment">recess appointment</a> of Merritt and <a href="/wiki/Silas_W._Burt" title="Silas W. Burt">Silas W. Burt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom370.E2.80.93371Reeves1975136.E2.80.93137_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom370.E2.80.93371Reeves1975136.E2.80.93137-78"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>f<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes again offered Arthur the position of <a href="/wiki/Consul_(representative)" title="Consul (representative)">consul general</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Face_(sociological_concept)" title="Face (sociological concept)">face-saving</a> consolation; Arthur again declined, as Hayes knew he probably would.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom354_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom354-81"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> Conkling opposed the confirmation of Merritt and Burt when the Senate reconvened in February 1879, but Merritt was approved by a vote of 31–25, as was Burt by 31–19, giving Hayes his most significant civil service reform victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom382.E2.80.93384Reeves1975138.E2.80.93148_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom382.E2.80.93384Reeves1975138.E2.80.93148-82"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur immediately took advantage of the resulting free time to work for the election of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Cooper_(mayor)" title="Edward Cooper (mayor)">Edward Cooper</a> as New York City's next mayor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe85_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe85-83"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> In the state elections of 1879, he and Conkling worked to ensure that the Republican nominees for state offices would be men of Conkling's faction, who had become known as Stalwarts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975153.E2.80.93155Peskin704_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975153.E2.80.93155Peskin704-84"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> They were successful, but narrowly, as Cornell was nominated for governor by a vote of 234–216.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975153.E2.80.93155Peskin704_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975153.E2.80.93155Peskin704-84"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur and Conkling campaigned vigorously for the Stalwart ticket and, owing partly to a splintering of the Democratic vote, were victorious.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975153.E2.80.93155Howe96.E2.80.9399_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975153.E2.80.93155Howe96.E2.80.9399-85"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur and the machine had rebuked Hayes and their intra-party rivals, but Arthur had only a few days to enjoy his triumph when, on January&#160;12, 1880, his wife died suddenly while he was in Albany organizing the political agenda for the coming year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975158.E2.80.93159Karabell38.E2.80.9339_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975158.E2.80.93159Karabell38.E2.80.9339-86"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur felt devastated, and perhaps guilty, and never remarried.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe98.E2.80.9399Karabell38.E2.80.9339_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe98.E2.80.9399Karabell38.E2.80.9339-87"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1880">Election of 1880</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Election of 1880">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1880" title="United States presidential election, 1880">United States presidential election, 1880</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1880RepublicanPoster.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/1880RepublicanPoster.png/250px-1880RepublicanPoster.png" width="250" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/1880RepublicanPoster.png/375px-1880RepublicanPoster.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/1880RepublicanPoster.png/500px-1880RepublicanPoster.png 2x" data-file-width="984" data-file-height="680" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1880RepublicanPoster.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Garfield–Arthur campaign poster</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Conkling and his fellow Stalwarts, including Arthur, wished to follow up their 1879 success at the <a href="/wiki/1880_Republican_National_Convention" title="1880 Republican National Convention">1880 Republican National Convention</a> by securing the nomination for their ally, ex-President Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975160.E2.80.93165_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975160.E2.80.93165-88"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Their opponents in the Republican party, known as <a href="/wiki/Half-Breed_(politics)" title="Half-Breed (politics)">Half-Breeds</a>, concentrated their efforts on <a href="/wiki/James_G._Blaine" title="James G. Blaine">James G. Blaine</a>, a Senator from Maine who was more amenable to civil service reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975160.E2.80.93165_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975160.E2.80.93165-88"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Neither candidate commanded a majority of delegates and, deadlocked after thirty-six ballots, the convention turned to a <a href="/wiki/Dark_horse" title="Dark horse">dark horse</a>, James A. Garfield, an Ohio Congressman and Civil War General who was neither Stalwart nor Half-Breed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975177.E2.80.93178Howe107.E2.80.93108Karabell39.E2.80.9340_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975177.E2.80.93178Howe107.E2.80.93108Karabell39.E2.80.9340-89"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield and his supporters knew they would face a difficult election without the support of the New York Stalwarts and decided to offer one of them the vice presidential nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell41Reeves1975178_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell41Reeves1975178-90"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Levi_P._Morton" title="Levi P. Morton">Levi P. Morton</a>, the first choice of Garfield's supporters, consulted with Conkling, who advised him to decline, which he did.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe107.E2.80.93108_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe107.E2.80.93108-91"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> They next approached Arthur, and Conkling advised him to also reject the nomination, believing the Republicans would lose.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975179.E2.80.93181_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975179.E2.80.93181-92"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur thought otherwise and accepted. According to a purported eyewitness account by journalist William C. Hudson, Conkling and Arthur argued, with Arthur telling Conkling, "The office of the Vice-President is a greater honor than I ever dreamed of attaining."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975179.E2.80.93181_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975179.E2.80.93181-92"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>g<span>]</span></a></sup> Conkling eventually relented, and campaigned for the <a href="/wiki/Ticket_(election)" title="Ticket (election)">ticket</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975190.E2.80.93194_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975190.E2.80.93194-96"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As expected, the election was close. The Democratic nominee, General <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott_Hancock" title="Winfield Scott Hancock">Winfield Scott Hancock</a>, was popular and, having avoided taking definitive positions on most issues of the day, he had not offended any pivotal constituencies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJordan292.E2.80.93305_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJordan292.E2.80.93305-97"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> As Republicans had done since the end of the Civil War, Garfield and Arthur initially focused their campaign on the "<a href="/wiki/Bloody_shirt" title="Bloody shirt" class="mw-redirect">bloody shirt</a>"—the idea that returning Democrats to office would undo the victory of the Civil War and reward <a href="/wiki/Secession_in_the_United_States" title="Secession in the United States">secessionists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975194.E2.80.93196Jordan294.E2.80.93295_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975194.E2.80.93196Jordan294.E2.80.93295-98"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> With the war fifteen years in the past and Union generals at the head of both tickets, the tactic was less effective than the Republicans hoped.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975194.E2.80.93196Jordan294.E2.80.93295_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975194.E2.80.93196Jordan294.E2.80.93295-98"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Realizing this, they adjusted their approach to claim that Democrats would lower the country's <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">protective tariff</a>, which would allow cheaper manufactured goods to be imported from Europe, and thereby put thousands out of work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975196.E2.80.93197Jordan297.E2.80.93302_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975196.E2.80.93197Jordan297.E2.80.93302-99"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> This argument struck home in the swing states of New York and Indiana, where many were employed in manufacturing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975196.E2.80.93197Jordan297.E2.80.93302_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975196.E2.80.93197Jordan297.E2.80.93302-99"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> Hancock did not help his own cause when, in an attempt to remain neutral on the tariff, he said that "[t]he tariff question is a local question", which only served to make him appear uninformed about an important issue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975196Jordan301_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975196Jordan301-100"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Candidates for high office did not personally campaign in those days, but Arthur played a part in the campaign in his usual fashion: raising money.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975198.E2.80.93202_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975198.E2.80.93202-101"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> The funds were crucial in the close election, and his home state of New York was crucial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975203.E2.80.93204_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975203.E2.80.93204-102"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republicans carried New York by 20,000 votes and, in an election with the largest turnout of qualified voters ever recorded—78.4%—they won the nationwide popular vote by just 7,018 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975203.E2.80.93204_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975203.E2.80.93204-102"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">electoral college</a> result was more decisive—214 to 155—and Garfield and Arthur were elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975203.E2.80.93204_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975203.E2.80.93204-102"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vice_presidency">Vice presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Vice presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Chester_A._Arthur" title="Inauguration of Chester A. Arthur">Inauguration of Chester A. Arthur</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CAArthur-oathofoffice.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A group of men, one with his hand raised" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/CAArthur-oathofoffice.jpg/220px-CAArthur-oathofoffice.jpg" width="220" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/CAArthur-oathofoffice.jpg/330px-CAArthur-oathofoffice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/CAArthur-oathofoffice.jpg/440px-CAArthur-oathofoffice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="837" data-file-height="941" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CAArthur-oathofoffice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arthur taking the oath of office as administered by Judge <a href="/wiki/John_R._Brady" title="John R. Brady">John R. Brady</a> at Arthur's home in New York City, September&#160;20, 1881</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the election, Arthur worked in vain to persuade Garfield to fill certain positions with his fellow New York Stalwarts—especially that of the Secretary of the Treasury; the Stalwart machine received a further rebuke when Garfield appointed Blaine, Conkling's arch-enemy, as Secretary of State.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975205.E2.80.93207_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975205.E2.80.93207-103"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> The running mates, never close, detached as Garfield continued to freeze out the Stalwarts from his patronage. Arthur's status in the administration diminished when, a month before inauguration day, he gave a speech before reporters suggesting the election in Indiana, a <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">swing state</a>, had been won by illegal voting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975213.E2.80.93216Karabell52.E2.80.9353_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975213.E2.80.93216Karabell52.E2.80.9353-104"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield ultimately appointed a Stalwart, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Lemuel_James" title="Thomas Lemuel James">Thomas Lemuel James</a>, to be Postmaster General, but the cabinet fight and Arthur's ill-considered speech left the President and Vice President clearly estranged when they took office on March&#160;4, 1881.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975216.E2.80.93219Karabell54.E2.80.9356_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975216.E2.80.93219Karabell54.E2.80.9356-105"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Senate in the <a href="/wiki/47th_United_States_Congress" title="47th United States Congress">47th United States Congress</a> was divided among 37 Republicans, 37 Democrats, one independent (<a href="/wiki/David_Davis_(Supreme_Court_justice)" title="David Davis (Supreme Court justice)">David Davis</a>) who <a href="/wiki/Congressional_caucus" title="Congressional caucus">caucused</a> with the Democrats, one <a href="/wiki/Readjuster_Party" title="Readjuster Party">Readjuster</a> (<a href="/wiki/William_Mahone" title="William Mahone">William Mahone</a>), and four vacancies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975220.E2.80.93223_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975220.E2.80.93223-106"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> Immediately, the Democrats attempted to organize the Senate, knowing that the vacancies would soon be filled by Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975220.E2.80.93223_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975220.E2.80.93223-106"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> As vice president, Arthur cast <a href="/wiki/List_of_tie-breaking_votes_cast_by_Vice_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of tie-breaking votes cast by Vice Presidents of the United States">tie-breaking votes</a> in favor of the Republicans when Mahone opted to join their caucus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975220.E2.80.93223_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975220.E2.80.93223-106"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> Even so, the Senate remained deadlocked for two months over Garfield's nominations because of Conkling's opposition to some of them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975223.E2.80.93230_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975223.E2.80.93230-107"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> Just before going into recess in May 1881, the situation became more complicated when Conkling and the other Senator from New York, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_C._Platt" title="Thomas C. Platt">Thomas C. Platt</a>, resigned in protest of Garfield's continuing opposition to their faction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975230.E2.80.93233_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975230.E2.80.93233-108"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With the Senate in recess, Arthur had no duties in Washington and returned to New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975233.E2.80.93237Howe147.E2.80.93149_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975233.E2.80.93237Howe147.E2.80.93149-109"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> Once there, he traveled with Conkling to <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany</a>, where the former Senator hoped for a quick re-election to the Senate, and with it, a defeat for the Garfield administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975233.E2.80.93237Howe147.E2.80.93149_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975233.E2.80.93237Howe147.E2.80.93149-109"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>h<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republican majority in the state legislature was divided on the question, to Conkling and Platt's surprise, and an intense campaign in the state house ensued.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975233.E2.80.93237Howe147.E2.80.93149_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975233.E2.80.93237Howe147.E2.80.93149-109"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>i<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>While in Albany on July 2, Arthur learned that Garfield had been shot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975233.E2.80.93237Howe147.E2.80.93149_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975233.E2.80.93237Howe147.E2.80.93149-109"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> The assassin, <a href="/wiki/Charles_J._Guiteau" title="Charles J. Guiteau">Charles J. Guiteau</a>, was a deranged office-seeker who believed that Garfield's successor would appoint him to a patronage job; indeed, he proclaimed to onlookers: "I am a Stalwart, and Arthur will be President!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell59Reeves1975237_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell59Reeves1975237-112"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite claims to the contrary, Guiteau was proven mentally unstable and unconnected with the Vice President.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975238.E2.80.93241Doenecke53.E2.80.9354_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975238.E2.80.93241Doenecke53.E2.80.9354-113"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>More troubling was the lack of legal guidance on <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_line_of_succession" title="United States presidential line of succession">presidential succession</a>: as Garfield lingered near death, no one was sure who, if anyone, could exercise presidential authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975241.E2.80.93243Howe152.E2.80.93154_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975241.E2.80.93243Howe152.E2.80.93154-114"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Also, after Conkling's resignation, the Senate had adjourned without electing a <i><a href="/wiki/President_pro_tempore_of_the_United_States_Senate" title="President pro tempore of the United States Senate">president pro tempore</a>,</i> who would normally follow Arthur in the succession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975241.E2.80.93243Howe152.E2.80.93154_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975241.E2.80.93243Howe152.E2.80.93154-114"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur was reluctant to be seen acting as president while Garfield lived, and for the next two months there was a void of authority in the executive office, with Garfield too weak to carry out his duties, and Arthur reluctant to assume them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975244.E2.80.93248Karabell61.E2.80.9363_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975244.E2.80.93248Karabell61.E2.80.9363-115"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Through the summer, Arthur refused to travel to Washington and was at his <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur_Home" title="Chester A. Arthur Home">Lexington Avenue home</a> when, on the night of September&#160;19, he learned that Garfield had died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975244.E2.80.93248Karabell61.E2.80.9363_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975244.E2.80.93248Karabell61.E2.80.9363-115"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Judge <a href="/wiki/John_R._Brady" title="John R. Brady">John R. Brady</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Supreme_Court" title="New York Supreme Court">New York Supreme Court</a> administered the <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States" title="Oath of office of the President of the United States">oath of office</a> in Arthur's home at 2:15&#160;a.m. the following day. He boarded a train for the nation's capital two days later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975244.E2.80.93248Karabell61.E2.80.9363_115-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975244.E2.80.93248Karabell61.E2.80.9363-115"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Before leaving New York, he ensured the presidential line of succession by preparing and mailing to the White House a proclamation calling for a Senate special session. This step ensured that the Senate had legal authority to convene immediately and choose a Senate president pro tempore, who would be able to assume the presidency if Arthur died. Once in Washington he destroyed the mailed proclamation and issued a formal call for a special session.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975247.E2.80.93248_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975247.E2.80.93248-116"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
