<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_1881.E2.80.931885">Presidency 1881–1885</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Presidency 1881–1885">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur-cabinet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Drawing of a group of men looking at another man" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Arthur-cabinet.jpg/250px-Arthur-cabinet.jpg" width="250" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Arthur-cabinet.jpg/375px-Arthur-cabinet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Arthur-cabinet.jpg/500px-Arthur-cabinet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="797" data-file-height="486" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur-cabinet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>On the threshold of office, what have we to expect of him?</i><br />
In an 1881 <i><a href="/wiki/Puck_(magazine)" title="Puck (magazine)">Puck</a></i> cartoon, Arthur faces the <a href="/wiki/James_Garfield#Administration_and_Cabinet" title="James Garfield" class="mw-redirect">presidential cabinet</a> after President Garfield was shot.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taking_office">Taking office</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Taking office">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Arriving in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> on September&#160;22, Arthur's oath of office was replicated, this time before Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Morrison_R._Waite" title="Morrison R. Waite" class="mw-redirect">Morrison R. Waite</a>, to assure procedural compliance <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke53.E2.80.9354Reeves1975248_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke53.E2.80.9354Reeves1975248-117"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> He initially took up residence at the home of Senator <a href="/wiki/John_P._Jones" title="John P. Jones">John P. Jones</a>, pending significant remodeling at the White House that he ordered; this included the addition of an elaborate fifty-foot glass screen made by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Comfort_Tiffany" title="Louis Comfort Tiffany">Louis Comfort Tiffany</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975252.E2.80.93253.2C_268.E2.80.93269_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975252.E2.80.93253.2C_268.E2.80.93269-118"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> His sister, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Arthur_McElroy" title="Mary Arthur McElroy">Mary Arthur McElroy</a>, served as White House hostess for her widowed brother;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975252.E2.80.93253.2C_268.E2.80.93269_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975252.E2.80.93253.2C_268.E2.80.93269-118"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur became Washington's most eligible bachelor and his social life became the subject of rumors, though romantically, he remained singularly devoted to the memory of his late wife.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975275.E2.80.93276_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975275.E2.80.93276-119"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> His son, Chester Jr., was then a freshman at <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> and his daughter, Nell, stayed in New York with a <a href="/wiki/Governess" title="Governess">governess</a> until 1882; when she arrived, Arthur shielded her from the intrusive press as much as he could.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975275.E2.80.93276_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975275.E2.80.93276-119"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Arthur quickly came into conflict with Garfield's cabinet, most of whom represented his opposition within the party. He asked the cabinet members to remain until December, when Congress would reconvene, but Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/William_Windom" title="William Windom">William Windom</a> submitted his resignation in October to enter a Senate race in his home state of Minnesota.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe160Reeves1975254_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe160Reeves1975254-120"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur replaced him with <a href="/wiki/Charles_J._Folger" title="Charles J. Folger">Charles J. Folger</a>, his friend and fellow New York Stalwart.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe160Reeves1975254_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe160Reeves1975254-120"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Wayne_MacVeagh" title="Wayne MacVeagh">Wayne MacVeagh</a> was next to resign, believing that, as a reformer, he had no place in an Arthur cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe161Reeves1975254.E2.80.93255_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe161Reeves1975254.E2.80.93255-121"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite Arthur's personal appeal to remain, MacVeagh resigned in December 1881 and Arthur replaced him with <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_H._Brewster" title="Benjamin H. Brewster">Benjamin H. Brewster</a>, a Philadelphia lawyer and machine politician reputed to have reformist leanings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe161Reeves1975254.E2.80.93255_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe161Reeves1975254.E2.80.93255-121"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Blaine, nemesis of the Stalwart faction, remained Secretary of State until Congress reconvened, then departed immediately.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe160.E2.80.93161Reeves1975255.E2.80.93257_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe160.E2.80.93161Reeves1975255.E2.80.93257-122"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Conkling expected Arthur to appoint him in Blaine's place, but the President chose <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Theodore_Frelinghuysen" title="Frederick Theodore Frelinghuysen">Frederick T. Frelinghuysen</a> of New Jersey, a Stalwart recommended by ex-President Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe160.E2.80.93161Reeves1975255.E2.80.93257_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe160.E2.80.93161Reeves1975255.E2.80.93257-122"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Frelinghuysen advised Arthur not to fill any future vacancies with Stalwarts, but when Postmaster General James resigned in January 1882, Arthur selected <a href="/wiki/Timothy_O._Howe" title="Timothy O. Howe">Timothy O. Howe</a>, a Wisconsin Stalwart.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe162.E2.80.93163Reeves1975257.E2.80.93258_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe162.E2.80.93163Reeves1975257.E2.80.93258-123"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> Navy Secretary <a href="/wiki/William_H._Hunt" title="William H. Hunt">William H. Hunt</a> was next to resign, in April 1882, and Arthur attempted a more balanced approach by appointing <a href="/wiki/William_E._Chandler" title="William E. Chandler">William E. Chandler</a> to the post, on Blaine's recommendation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe162.E2.80.93163Reeves1975257.E2.80.93258_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe162.E2.80.93163Reeves1975257.E2.80.93258-123"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> Finally, when Interior Secretary <a href="/wiki/Samuel_J._Kirkwood" title="Samuel J. Kirkwood">Samuel J. Kirkwood</a> resigned that same month, Arthur appointed <a href="/wiki/Henry_M._Teller" title="Henry M. Teller">Henry M. Teller</a>, a Colorado Stalwart to the office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe162.E2.80.93163Reeves1975257.E2.80.93258_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe162.E2.80.93163Reeves1975257.E2.80.93258-123"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> Of the Cabinet members Arthur had inherited from Garfield, only Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/Robert_Todd_Lincoln" title="Robert Todd Lincoln">Robert Todd Lincoln</a> remained for the entirety of Arthur's term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe162.E2.80.93163Reeves1975257.E2.80.93258_123-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe162.E2.80.93163Reeves1975257.E2.80.93258-123"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_service_reform">Civil service reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Civil service reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chester_A._Arthur_by_Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Portrait of a man with a tremendous mustache" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Chester_A._Arthur_by_Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling.JPG/220px-Chester_A._Arthur_by_Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling.JPG" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Chester_A._Arthur_by_Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling.JPG/330px-Chester_A._Arthur_by_Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Chester_A._Arthur_by_Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling.JPG/440px-Chester_A._Arthur_by_Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1705" data-file-height="2013" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chester_A._Arthur_by_Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ole_Peter_Hansen_Balling" title="Ole Peter Hansen Balling">Ole Peter Hansen Balling</a>'s 1881 portrait of Chester A. Arthur</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1870s, a <a href="/wiki/Star_route_scandal" title="Star route scandal">scandal</a> was exposed, in which contractors for <a href="/wiki/Star_routes" title="Star routes">star postal routes</a> were greatly overpaid for their services with the connivance of government officials (including Second Assistant Postal Secretary <a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Brady" title="Thomas J. Brady">Thomas J. Brady</a> and former Senator <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Wallace_Dorsey" title="Stephen Wallace Dorsey">Stephen Wallace Dorsey</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke93.E2.80.9395Reeves1975297.E2.80.93298_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke93.E2.80.9395Reeves1975297.E2.80.93298-124"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> Reformers feared Arthur, as a former supporter of the spoils system, would not commit to continuing the investigation into the scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke93.E2.80.9395Reeves1975297.E2.80.93298_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke93.E2.80.9395Reeves1975297.E2.80.93298-124"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> But Arthur's Attorney General, Brewster, did in fact continue the investigations begun by MacVeigh, and hired notable Democratic lawyers William W. Ker and <a href="/wiki/Richard_T._Merrick" title="Richard T. Merrick">Richard T. Merrick</a> to strengthen the prosecution team and forestall the skeptics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975299.E2.80.93300Howe182_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975299.E2.80.93300Howe182-125"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Arthur had worked closely with Dorsey before his presidency, once in office he supported the investigation and forced the resignation of officials suspected in the scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975299.E2.80.93300Howe182_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975299.E2.80.93300Howe182-125"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> An 1882 trial of the ringleaders resulted in convictions for two minor conspirators and a <a href="/wiki/Hung_jury" title="Hung jury">hung jury</a> for the rest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975301.E2.80.93302Howe185.E2.80.93189_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975301.E2.80.93302Howe185.E2.80.93189-126"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> After a juror came forward with allegations that the defendants attempted to bribe him, the judge set aside the guilty verdicts and granted a new trial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975301.E2.80.93302Howe185.E2.80.93189_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975301.E2.80.93302Howe185.E2.80.93189-126"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Before the second trial began, Arthur removed five federal office holders who were sympathetic with the defense, including a former Senator.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975303.E2.80.93305Howe189.E2.80.93193_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975303.E2.80.93305Howe189.E2.80.93193-127"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> The second trial began in December 1882 and lasted until July 1883 and, again, did not result in a guilty verdict.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975303.E2.80.93305Howe189.E2.80.93193_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975303.E2.80.93305Howe189.E2.80.93193-127"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Failure to obtain a conviction tarnished the administration's image, but Arthur did succeed in putting a stop to the fraud.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975303.E2.80.93305Howe189.E2.80.93193_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975303.E2.80.93305Howe189.E2.80.93193-127"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Garfield's assassination by a deranged office seeker amplified the public demand for civil service reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975320.E2.80.93324Doenecke96.E2.80.9397Theriault52.E2.80.9353.2C_56_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975320.E2.80.93324Doenecke96.E2.80.9397Theriault52.E2.80.9353.2C_56-128"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Both Democratic and Republican leaders realized that they could attract the votes of reformers by turning against the spoils system and, by 1882, a bipartisan effort began in favor of reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975320.E2.80.93324Doenecke96.E2.80.9397Theriault52.E2.80.9353.2C_56_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975320.E2.80.93324Doenecke96.E2.80.9397Theriault52.E2.80.9353.2C_56-128"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1880, Democratic Senator <a href="/wiki/George_H._Pendleton" title="George H. Pendleton">George H. Pendleton</a> of Ohio introduced legislation that required selection of civil servants based on merit as determined by an <a href="/wiki/Civil_service_examination" title="Civil service examination">examination</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975320.E2.80.93324Doenecke96.E2.80.9397Theriault52.E2.80.9353.2C_56_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975320.E2.80.93324Doenecke96.E2.80.9397Theriault52.E2.80.9353.2C_56-128"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> In his first <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_address" title="State of the Union address" class="mw-redirect">annual presidential address to Congress</a> in 1881, Arthur requested civil service reform legislation and Pendleton again introduced his bill, but Congress did not pass it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975320.E2.80.93324Doenecke96.E2.80.9397Theriault52.E2.80.9353.2C_56_128-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975320.E2.80.93324Doenecke96.E2.80.9397Theriault52.E2.80.9353.2C_56-128"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Republicans lost seats in the 1882 congressional elections, in which Democrats campaigned on the reform issue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke99.E2.80.93100Theriault57.E2.80.9363_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke99.E2.80.93100Theriault57.E2.80.9363-129"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result, the <a href="/wiki/Lame_duck_session" title="Lame duck session" class="mw-redirect">lame duck session</a> of Congress was more amenable to civil service reform; the Senate approved Pendleton's bill 38–5 and the House soon concurred by a vote of 155–47.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975324Doenecke101.E2.80.93102_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975324Doenecke101.E2.80.93102-130"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur signed the Pendleton Civil Service Reform Act into law on January&#160;16, 1883.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975324Doenecke101.E2.80.93102_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975324Doenecke101.E2.80.93102-130"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> In just two years' time, an unrepentant Stalwart had become the president who ushered in long-awaited civil service reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975324Doenecke101.E2.80.93102_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975324Doenecke101.E2.80.93102-130"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At first, the act applied only to 10% of federal jobs and, without proper implementation by the president, it could have gone no further.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975325.E2.80.93327Doenecke102.E2.80.93104_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975325.E2.80.93327Doenecke102.E2.80.93104-131"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Even after he signed the act into law, its proponents doubted Arthur's commitment to reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975325.E2.80.93327Doenecke102.E2.80.93104_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975325.E2.80.93327Doenecke102.E2.80.93104-131"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> To their surprise, he acted quickly to appoint the members of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_Service_Commission" title="United States Civil Service Commission">Civil Service Commission</a> that the law created, naming reformers <a href="/wiki/Dorman_Bridgeman_Eaton" title="Dorman Bridgeman Eaton">Dorman Bridgeman Eaton</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Milton_Gregory" title="John Milton Gregory">John Milton Gregory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leroy_D._Thoman" title="Leroy D. Thoman">Leroy D. Thoman</a> as commissioners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975325.E2.80.93327Doenecke102.E2.80.93104_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975325.E2.80.93327Doenecke102.E2.80.93104-131"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> The chief examiner, <a href="/wiki/Silas_W._Burt" title="Silas W. Burt">Silas W. Burt</a>, was a long-time reformer who had been Arthur's opponent when the two men worked at the New York Customs House.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe209.E2.80.93210_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe209.E2.80.93210-132"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> The commission issued its first rules in May 1883; by 1884, half of all postal officials and three-quarters of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Customs_Service" title="United States Customs Service">Customs Service</a> jobs were to be awarded by merit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe209.E2.80.93210_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe209.E2.80.93210-132"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> That year, Arthur expressed satisfaction with the new system, praising its effectiveness "in securing competent and faithful public servants and in protecting the appointing officers of the Government from the pressure of personal importunity and from the labor of examining the claims and pretensions of rival candidates for public employment."<sup id="cite_ref-Arthur_1884_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arthur_1884-133"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surplus_and_the_tariff">Surplus and the tariff</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Surplus and the tariff">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -48px; left: -38px; width: 310px"><a href="/wiki/File:ARTHUR,_Chester_A-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Arthur as president" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/ARTHUR%2C_Chester_A-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/310px-ARTHUR%2C_Chester_A-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="310" height="414" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/ARTHUR%2C_Chester_A-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/465px-ARTHUR%2C_Chester_A-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/ARTHUR%2C_Chester_A-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/620px-ARTHUR%2C_Chester_A-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8216" data-file-height="10984" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ARTHUR,_Chester_A-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:ARTHUR, Chester A-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:ARTHUR, Chester A-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Arthur as president</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With high revenue held over from wartime taxes, the federal government had collected more than it spent since 1866; by 1882 the surplus reached $145 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975328.E2.80.93329Doenecke168_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975328.E2.80.93329Doenecke168-134"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Opinions varied on how to <a href="/wiki/Balanced_budget" title="Balanced budget">balance the budget</a>; the Democrats wished to lower tariffs, in order to reduce revenues and the cost of imported goods, while Republicans believed that high tariffs ensured high wages in manufacturing and mining. They preferred the government spend more on <a href="/wiki/Internal_improvements" title="Internal improvements">internal improvements</a> and reduce <a href="/wiki/Excise" title="Excise">excise</a> taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975328.E2.80.93329Doenecke168_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975328.E2.80.93329Doenecke168-134"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur agreed with his party, and in 1882 called for the abolition of excise taxes on everything except liquor, as well as a simplification of the complex tariff structure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975330.E2.80.93333Doenecke169.E2.80.93171_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975330.E2.80.93333Doenecke169.E2.80.93171-135"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> In May of that year, Representative <a href="/wiki/William_D._Kelley" title="William D. Kelley">William D. Kelley</a> of Pennsylvania introduced a bill to establish a tariff commission;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975330.E2.80.93333Doenecke169.E2.80.93171_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975330.E2.80.93333Doenecke169.E2.80.93171-135"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> the bill passed and Arthur signed it into law but appointed mostly <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionists</a> to the committee. Republicans were pleased with the committee's make-up but were surprised when, in December 1882, they submitted a report to Congress calling for tariff cuts averaging between 20 and 25%. The commission's recommendations were ignored, however, as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Ways_and_Means" title="United States House Committee on Ways and Means">House Ways and Means Committee</a>, dominated by protectionists, provided a 10% reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975330.E2.80.93333Doenecke169.E2.80.93171_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975330.E2.80.93333Doenecke169.E2.80.93171-135"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> After conference with the Senate, <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1883" title="Tariff of 1883">the bill that emerged</a> only reduced tariffs by an average of 1.47%. The bill passed both houses narrowly on March&#160;3, 1883, the last full day of the 47th Congress; Arthur signed the measure into law, with no effect on the surplus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975334.E2.80.93335_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975334.E2.80.93335-136"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Congress attempted to balance the budget from the other side of the ledger, with increased spending on the 1882 <a href="/wiki/Rivers_and_Harbors_Act" title="Rivers and Harbors Act">Rivers and Harbors Act</a> in the unprecedented amount of $19 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975280.E2.80.93282Doenecke81_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975280.E2.80.93282Doenecke81-137"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> While Arthur was not opposed to internal improvements, the scale of the bill disturbed him, as did its narrow focus on "particular localities," rather than projects that benefited a larger part of the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975280.E2.80.93282Doenecke81_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975280.E2.80.93282Doenecke81-137"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> On August&#160;1, 1882, Arthur vetoed the bill to widespread popular acclaim;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975280.E2.80.93282Doenecke81_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975280.E2.80.93282Doenecke81-137"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> in his veto message, his principal objection was that it appropriated funds for purposes "not for the common defense or general welfare, and which do not promote commerce among the States."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975281_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975281-138"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress <a href="/wiki/Veto_override" title="Veto override" class="mw-redirect">overrode</a> his veto the next day<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975280.E2.80.93282Doenecke81_137-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975280.E2.80.93282Doenecke81-137"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> and the new law reduced the surplus by $19 million. Republicans considered the law a success at the time, but later concluded that it contributed to their loss of seats in the elections of 1882.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe196.E2.80.93197Reeves1975281.E2.80.93282Karabell90_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe196.E2.80.93197Reeves1975281.E2.80.93282Karabell90-139"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs_and_immigration">Foreign affairs and immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Foreign affairs and immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Chinese man sitting outside a locked gate" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg/220px-The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg" width="220" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg/330px-The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg/440px-The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg 2x" data-file-width="976" data-file-height="1060" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A political cartoon from 1882, criticizing <a href="/wiki/Chinese_exclusion" title="Chinese exclusion" class="mw-redirect">Chinese exclusion</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">Garfield administration</a>, Secretary of State James G. Blaine attempted to invigorate United States diplomacy in Latin America, urging reciprocal trade agreements and offering to mediate disputes among the Latin American nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke55.E2.80.9357Reeves1975284.E2.80.93289_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke55.E2.80.9357Reeves1975284.E2.80.93289-140"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> Blaine, venturing a greater involvement in affairs south of the Rio Grande, proposed a Pan-American conference in 1882 to discuss trade and an end to the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Pacific" title="War of the Pacific">War of the Pacific</a> being fought by <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, Chile, and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke55.E2.80.9357Reeves1975284.E2.80.93289_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke55.E2.80.9357Reeves1975284.E2.80.93289-140"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> Blaine did not remain in office long enough to see the effort through, and when <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Theodore_Frelinghuysen" title="Frederick Theodore Frelinghuysen">Frederick T. Frelinghuysen</a> replaced him at the end of 1881, the conference efforts lapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke129.E2.80.93132Reeves1975289.E2.80.93293Bastert653.E2.80.93671_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke129.E2.80.93132Reeves1975289.E2.80.93293Bastert653.E2.80.93671-141"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Frelinghuysen also discontinued Blaine's peace efforts in the War of the Pacific, fearing that the United States might be drawn into the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke129.E2.80.93132Reeves1975289.E2.80.93293Bastert653.E2.80.93671_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke129.E2.80.93132Reeves1975289.E2.80.93293Bastert653.E2.80.93671-141"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur and Frelinghuysen continued Blaine's efforts to encourage trade among the nations of the Western Hemisphere; a treaty with Mexico providing for reciprocal tariff reductions was signed in 1882 and approved by the Senate in 1884.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke173.E2.80.93175Reeves1975398.E2.80.93399.2C_409_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke173.E2.80.93175Reeves1975398.E2.80.93399.2C_409-142"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> Legislation required to bring the treaty into force failed in the House, however, rendering it a <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dead_letter" class="extiw" title="wikt:dead letter">dead letter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke173.E2.80.93175Reeves1975398.E2.80.93399.2C_409_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke173.E2.80.93175Reeves1975398.E2.80.93399.2C_409-142"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> Similar efforts at reciprocal trade treaties with <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_West_Indies" title="Spanish West Indies">Spain's American colonies</a> were defeated by February 1885, and an existing reciprocity treaty with the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Kingdom of Hawaii</a> was allowed to lapse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke175.E2.80.93178Reeves1975398.E2.80.93399.2C_407.E2.80.93410_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke175.E2.80.93178Reeves1975398.E2.80.93399.2C_407.E2.80.93410-143"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The 47th Congress spent a great deal of time on immigration, and at times was in accord with Arthur.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe168.E2.80.93169Doenecke81_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe168.E2.80.93169Doenecke81-144"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> In July 1882 Congress easily passed a bill regulating steamships that carried immigrants to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe168.E2.80.93169Doenecke81_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe168.E2.80.93169Doenecke81-144"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> To their surprise, Arthur vetoed it and requested revisions, which they made and which Arthur then approved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe168.E2.80.93169Doenecke81_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe168.E2.80.93169Doenecke81-144"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> He also signed in August of that year the <a href="/wiki/Immigration_Act_of_1882" title="Immigration Act of 1882">Immigration Act of 1882</a>, which levied a 50-cent tax on immigrants to the United States, and excluded from entry the <a href="/wiki/Mentally_ill" title="Mentally ill" class="mw-redirect">mentally ill</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_disability" title="Intellectual disability">intellectually disabled</a>, criminals, or any other person potentially dependent upon public assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchinson162Howe169_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchinson162Howe169-145"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A more contentious debate materialized over the status of Chinese immigrants; in January 1868, the Senate had ratified the <a href="/wiki/Burlingame_Treaty" title="Burlingame Treaty">Burlingame Treaty</a> with China, allowing an unrestricted flow of Chinese into the country. As the economy soured after the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1873" title="Panic of 1873">Panic of 1873</a>, Chinese immigrants were blamed for depressing workmen's wages; in reaction Congress in 1879 attempted to abrogate the 1868 treaty by passing the Chinese Exclusion Act, but President Hayes vetoed it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975277.E2.80.93278Hoogenboom387.E2.80.93389_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975277.E2.80.93278Hoogenboom387.E2.80.93389-146"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> Three years later, after China had agreed to treaty revisions, Congress tried again to exclude Chinese immigrants; Senator <a href="/wiki/John_Franklin_Miller_(senator)" title="John Franklin Miller (senator)">John F. Miller</a> of California introduced another Chinese Exclusion Act that denied Chinese immigrants United States citizenship and banned their immigration for a twenty-year period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975278.E2.80.93279Doenecke81.E2.80.9384_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975278.E2.80.93279Doenecke81.E2.80.9384-147"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> The bill passed the Senate and House by overwhelming margins, but this as well was vetoed by Arthur, who concluded the 20-year ban to be a breach of the renegotiated treaty of 1880. That treaty allowed only a "reasonable" suspension of immigration. Eastern newspapers praised the veto, while it was condemned in the Western states. Congress was unable to override the veto, but passed a new bill reducing the immigration ban to ten years. Although he still objected to this denial of citizenship to Chinese immigrants, Arthur acceded to the compromise measure, signing the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Exclusion_Act" title="Chinese Exclusion Act">Chinese Exclusion Act</a> into law on May&#160;6, 1882.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975278.E2.80.93279Doenecke81.E2.80.9384_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975278.E2.80.93279Doenecke81.E2.80.9384-147"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>j<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_reform">Naval reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Naval reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Squadron_of_Evolution_(Hart).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of four warships" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Squadron_of_Evolution_%28Hart%29.jpg/220px-Squadron_of_Evolution_%28Hart%29.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Squadron_of_Evolution_%28Hart%29.jpg/330px-Squadron_of_Evolution_%28Hart%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Squadron_of_Evolution_%28Hart%29.jpg/440px-Squadron_of_Evolution_%28Hart%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="738" data-file-height="569" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Squadron_of_Evolution_(Hart).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The "Squadron of Evolution" at anchor in 1889, after <i>Yorktown</i> had been added: <i>Chicago</i>, <i>Yorktown</i>, <i>Boston</i>, <i>Atlanta</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the years following the Civil War, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">American naval power</a> declined precipitously, shrinking from nearly 700 vessels to just 52, most of which were obsolete.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975337Doenecke145_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975337Doenecke145-149"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> The nation's military focus over the fifteen years before Garfield and Arthur's election had been on the <a href="/wiki/American_Indian_Wars" title="American Indian Wars">Indian wars</a> in the West, rather than the high seas, but as the region was increasingly pacified, many in Congress grew concerned at the poor state of the Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975338.E2.80.93341Doenecke145.E2.80.93147_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975338.E2.80.93341Doenecke145.E2.80.93147-150"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield's Secretary of the Navy, <a href="/wiki/William_H._Hunt" title="William H. Hunt">William H. Hunt</a>, advocated reform of the Navy and his successor, <a href="/wiki/William_E._Chandler" title="William E. Chandler">William E. Chandler</a>, appointed an advisory board to prepare a report on modernization.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke147.E2.80.93149_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke147.E2.80.93149-151"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Based on the suggestions in the report, Congress appropriated funds for the construction of three steel <a href="/wiki/Protected_cruiser" title="Protected cruiser">protected cruisers</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/USS_Atlanta_(1884)" title="USS Atlanta (1884)">Atlanta</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Boston_(1884)" title="USS Boston (1884)">Boston</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Chicago_(1885)" title="USS Chicago (1885)">Chicago</a></i>) and an armed dispatch-steamer (<i><a href="/wiki/USS_Dolphin_(PG-24)" title="USS Dolphin (PG-24)">Dolphin</a></i>), collectively known as the "ABCD Ships" or the "<a href="/wiki/Squadron_of_Evolution" title="Squadron of Evolution">Squadron of Evolution</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975342.E2.80.93343Abbot346.E2.80.93347_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975342.E2.80.93343Abbot346.E2.80.93347-152"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress also approved funds to rebuild four <a href="/wiki/Monitor_(warship)" title="Monitor (warship)">monitors</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/USS_Puritan_(BM-1)" title="USS Puritan (BM-1)">Puritan</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Amphitrite_(BM-2)" title="USS Amphitrite (BM-2)">Amphitrite</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Monadnock_(BM-3)" title="USS Monadnock (BM-3)">Monadnock</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Terror_(BM-4)" title="USS Terror (BM-4)">Terror</a>,</i>) which had lain uncompleted since 1877.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975342.E2.80.93343Abbot346.E2.80.93347_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975342.E2.80.93343Abbot346.E2.80.93347-152"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> The contracts to build the ABCD ships were all awarded to the low bidder, <a href="/wiki/John_Roach_%26_Sons" title="John Roach &amp; Sons">John Roach &amp; Sons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chester,_Pennsylvania" title="Chester, Pennsylvania">Chester</a>, Pennsylvania,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975343.E2.80.93345Doenecke149.E2.80.93151_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975343.E2.80.93345Doenecke149.E2.80.93151-153"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> even though Roach once employed Secretary Chandler as a lobbyist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975343.E2.80.93345Doenecke149.E2.80.93151_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975343.E2.80.93345Doenecke149.E2.80.93151-153"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> Democrats turned against the "New Navy" projects and, when they won control of the <a href="/wiki/48th_United_States_Congress" title="48th United States Congress">48th Congress</a>, refused to appropriate funds for seven more steel warships.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975343.E2.80.93345Doenecke149.E2.80.93151_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975343.E2.80.93345Doenecke149.E2.80.93151-153"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> Even without the additional ships, the state of the Navy improved when, after several construction delays, the last of the new ships entered service in 1889.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975349.E2.80.93350Doenecke152.E2.80.93153_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975349.E2.80.93350Doenecke152.E2.80.93153-154"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Civil rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Mahone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/William_Mahone.jpg/220px-William_Mahone.jpg" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/William_Mahone.jpg/330px-William_Mahone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/William_Mahone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="469" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Mahone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Readjuster Party leader <a href="/wiki/William_Mahone" title="William Mahone">William Mahone</a> pressed civil rights in Virginia.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Like his Republican predecessors, Arthur struggled with the question of how his party was to challenge the Democrats in <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">the South</a> and how, if at all, to protect the civil rights of black southerners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975306.E2.80.93308Doenecke105.E2.80.93108_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975306.E2.80.93308Doenecke105.E2.80.93108-155"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> Since the end of <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction Era of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Reconstruction</a>, conservative white Democrats (or "<a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Democrat" title="Bourbon Democrat">Bourbon Democrats</a>") had regained power in the South, and the Republican party dwindled rapidly as their primary supporters in the region, blacks, <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement_after_Reconstruction_era" title="Disfranchisement after Reconstruction era" class="mw-redirect">were disenfranchised</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975306.E2.80.93308Doenecke105.E2.80.93108_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975306.E2.80.93308Doenecke105.E2.80.93108-155"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> One crack in the <a href="/wiki/Solid_South" title="Solid South">solidly Democratic South</a> emerged with the growth of a new party, the <a href="/wiki/Readjuster_Party" title="Readjuster Party">Readjusters</a>, in Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975307.E2.80.93309Ayers46.E2.80.9347_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975307.E2.80.93309Ayers46.E2.80.9347-156"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Having won an election in that state on a platform of more education funding (for black and white schools alike) and abolition of the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(United_States)" title="Poll tax (United States)">poll tax</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pillory" title="Pillory">whipping post</a>, many northern Republicans saw the Readjusters as a more viable ally in the South than the moribund southern Republican party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975307.E2.80.93309Ayers46.E2.80.9347_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975307.E2.80.93309Ayers46.E2.80.9347-156"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur agreed, and directed the federal patronage in Virginia through the Readjusters rather than the Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975307.E2.80.93309Ayers46.E2.80.9347_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975307.E2.80.93309Ayers46.E2.80.9347-156"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> He followed the same pattern in other Southern states, forging coalitions with independents and <a href="/wiki/Greenback_Party" title="Greenback Party">Greenback Party</a> members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975307.E2.80.93309Ayers46.E2.80.9347_156-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975307.E2.80.93309Ayers46.E2.80.9347-156"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> Some black Republicans felt betrayed by the pragmatic gambit, but others (including <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a> and ex-Senator <a href="/wiki/Blanche_K._Bruce" title="Blanche K. Bruce" class="mw-redirect">Blanche K. Bruce</a>) endorsed the administration's actions, as the Southern independents had more liberal racial policies than the Democrats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975310.E2.80.93313_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975310.E2.80.93313-157"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur's coalition policy was only successful in Virginia, however, and by 1885 the Readjuster movement began to collapse with the election of a Democratic president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAyers47.E2.80.9348_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAyers47.E2.80.9348-158"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Other federal action on behalf of blacks was equally ineffective: when the Supreme Court struck down the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">Civil Rights Act of 1875</a> in <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Cases" title="Civil Rights Cases">an 1883 decision</a>, Arthur expressed his disagreement with the decision in a message to Congress, but was unable to persuade Congress to pass any new legislation in its place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke112.E2.80.93114_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke112.E2.80.93114-159"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur did, however, effectively intervene to overturn a <a href="/wiki/Court-martial" title="Court-martial">court-martial</a> ruling against a black <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">West Point</a> cadet, <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Chesnut_Whittaker" title="Johnson Chesnut Whittaker">Johnson Whittaker</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Judge_Advocate_General_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Judge Advocate General of the United States Army">Judge Advocate General of the Army</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_G._Swaim" title="David G. Swaim">David G. Swaim</a>, found the prosecution's case against Whittaker to be illegal and based on racism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarszalek.27.27.5B.5Bpassim.5D.5D.27.27_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarszalek.27.27.5B.5Bpassim.5D.5D.27.27-160"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> The administration faced a different challenge in the West, where the <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">LDS Church</a> was under government pressure to stop the practice of <a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">polygamy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Utah_Territory" title="Utah Territory">Utah Territory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke84.E2.80.9385_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke84.E2.80.9385-161"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> Garfield had believed polygamy was criminal behavior and was morally detrimental to family values, and Arthur's views were, for once, in line with his predecessor's.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke84.E2.80.9385_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke84.E2.80.9385-161"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1882, he signed the <a href="/wiki/Edmunds_Act" title="Edmunds Act">Edmunds Act</a> into law; the legislation made polygamy a federal crime, barring polygamists both from public office and the right to vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke84.E2.80.9385_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke84.E2.80.9385-161"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Native_American_policy">Native American policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Native American policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Arthur administration was challenged by changing relations with western <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">American Indian</a> tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke85.E2.80.9389_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke85.E2.80.9389-162"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> The Indian Wars were winding down, and public sentiment was shifting toward more favorable treatment of Native Americans. Arthur urged Congress to increase funding for Indian education, which it did in 1884, although not to the extent he wished.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9392Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9392Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363-163"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> He also favored a move to the <a href="/wiki/Allotment_Era" title="Allotment Era" class="mw-redirect">allotment system</a>, under which individual Native Americans, rather than tribes, would own land. Arthur was unable to convince Congress to adopt the idea during his administration but, in 1887, the <a href="/wiki/Dawes_Act" title="Dawes Act">Dawes Act</a> changed the law to favor such a system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9392Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9392Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363-163"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> The allotment system was favored by liberal reformers at the time, but eventually proved detrimental to Native Americans as most of their land was resold at low prices to white <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke91Stuart452.E2.80.93454_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke91Stuart452.E2.80.93454-164"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> During Arthur's presidency, settlers and cattle ranchers continued to encroach on Indian territory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9390Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9390Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363-165"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur initially resisted their efforts, but after Secretary of the Interior <a href="/wiki/Henry_M._Teller" title="Henry M. Teller">Henry M. Teller</a>, an opponent of allotment, assured him that the lands were not protected, Arthur opened up the <a href="/wiki/Crow_Creek_Reservation" title="Crow Creek Reservation" class="mw-redirect">Crow Creek Reservation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Dakota_Territory" title="Dakota Territory">Dakota Territory</a> to settlers by executive order in 1885.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9390Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9390Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363-165"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur's successor, <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a>, finding that title belonged to the Indians, revoked Arthur's order a few months later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9390Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke89.E2.80.9390Reeves1975362.E2.80.93363-165"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health.2C_travel.2C_and_1884_election">Health, travel, and 1884 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Health, travel, and 1884 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Chester_A._Arthur_Yellowstone_National_Park_Expedition_1883.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A group of men seated in a forest" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/President_Chester_A._Arthur_Yellowstone_National_Park_Expedition_1883.jpg/250px-President_Chester_A._Arthur_Yellowstone_National_Park_Expedition_1883.jpg" width="250" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/President_Chester_A._Arthur_Yellowstone_National_Park_Expedition_1883.jpg/375px-President_Chester_A._Arthur_Yellowstone_National_Park_Expedition_1883.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/President_Chester_A._Arthur_Yellowstone_National_Park_Expedition_1883.jpg/500px-President_Chester_A._Arthur_Yellowstone_National_Park_Expedition_1883.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3367" data-file-height="2315" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Chester_A._Arthur_Yellowstone_National_Park_Expedition_1883.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arthur on an expedition in <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a> along with <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sheridan" title="Philip Sheridan">Philip Sheridan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Todd_Lincoln" title="Robert Todd Lincoln">Robert Todd Lincoln</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shortly after becoming president, Arthur was diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Bright%27s_disease" title="Bright's disease">Bright's disease</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidney</a> ailment now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Nephritis" title="Nephritis">nephritis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975317.E2.80.93318Howe243.E2.80.93244_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975317.E2.80.93318Howe243.E2.80.93244-166"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> He attempted to keep his condition private, but by 1883 rumors of his illness began to circulate; he had become thinner and more aged in appearance, and struggled to keep the pace of the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975317.E2.80.93318Howe243.E2.80.93244_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975317.E2.80.93318Howe243.E2.80.93244-166"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> To rejuvenate his health outside the confines of Washington, Arthur and some political friends traveled to Florida in April 1883.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975355.E2.80.93359Howe244.E2.80.93246_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975355.E2.80.93359Howe244.E2.80.93246-167"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> The vacation had the opposite effect, and Arthur suffered from intense pain before returning to Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975355.E2.80.93359Howe244.E2.80.93246_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975355.E2.80.93359Howe244.E2.80.93246-167"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> Later that year, on the advice of Missouri Senator <a href="/wiki/George_Graham_Vest" title="George Graham Vest">George Graham Vest</a>, he visited <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975364.E2.80.93367Howe247.E2.80.93248_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975364.E2.80.93367Howe247.E2.80.93248-168"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> Reporters accompanied the presidential party, helping to publicize the new <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_National_Park_Service" title="History of the National Park Service">National Park system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975364.E2.80.93367Howe247.E2.80.93248_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975364.E2.80.93367Howe247.E2.80.93248-168"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> The Yellowstone trip was more beneficial to Arthur's health than his Florida excursion, and he returned to Washington refreshed after two months of travel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell124.E2.80.93125Reeves1975366.E2.80.93367_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell124.E2.80.93125Reeves1975366.E2.80.93367-169"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1884" title="United States presidential election, 1884">1884 presidential election</a> approached, James G. Blaine was considered the favorite for the Republican nomination, but Arthur, too, contemplated a run for a full term as president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975368.E2.80.93371Howe254.E2.80.93257_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975368.E2.80.93371Howe254.E2.80.93257-170"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> In the months leading up to the <a href="/wiki/1884_Republican_National_Convention" title="1884 Republican National Convention">1884 Republican National Convention</a>, however, Arthur began to realize that neither faction of the Republican party was prepared to give him their full support: the Half-Breeds were again solidly behind Blaine, while Stalwarts were undecided; some backed Arthur, with others considering Senator <a href="/wiki/John_A._Logan" title="John A. Logan">John A. Logan</a> of Illinois.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975368.E2.80.93371Howe254.E2.80.93257_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975368.E2.80.93371Howe254.E2.80.93257-170"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> Reform-minded Republicans, friendlier to Arthur after he endorsed civil service reform, were still not certain enough of his reform credentials to back him over Senator <a href="/wiki/George_F._Edmunds" title="George F. Edmunds">George F. Edmunds</a> of Vermont, who had long favored their cause.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975368.E2.80.93371Howe254.E2.80.93257_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975368.E2.80.93371Howe254.E2.80.93257-170"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> Business leaders supported him, as did Southern Republicans who owed their jobs to his control of the patronage, but by the time they began to rally around him, Arthur had decided against a serious campaign for the nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975373.E2.80.93375Doenecke181.E2.80.93182_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975373.E2.80.93375Doenecke181.E2.80.93182-171"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> He kept up a token effort, believing that to drop out would cast doubt on his actions in office and raise questions about his health, but by the time the convention began in June, his defeat was assured.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975373.E2.80.93375Doenecke181.E2.80.93182_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975373.E2.80.93375Doenecke181.E2.80.93182-171"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> Blaine led on the first ballot, and by the fourth ballot he had a majority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975380.E2.80.93381Howe264.E2.80.93265_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975380.E2.80.93381Howe264.E2.80.93265-172"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur telegraphed his congratulations to Blaine and accepted his defeat with equanimity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975380.E2.80.93381Howe264.E2.80.93265_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975380.E2.80.93381Howe264.E2.80.93265-172"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> He played no role in the 1884 campaign, which Blaine would later blame for his loss that November to the Democratic nominee, Grover Cleveland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975387.E2.80.93389Howe265.E2.80.93266_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975387.E2.80.93389Howe265.E2.80.93266-173"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_cabinet">Administration and cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Administration and cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Daniel_Huntington_-_Chester_Alan_Arthur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Portrait of a man in a fur coat" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Daniel_Huntington_-_Chester_Alan_Arthur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Daniel_Huntington_-_Chester_Alan_Arthur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="354" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Daniel_Huntington_-_Chester_Alan_Arthur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Daniel_Huntington_-_Chester_Alan_Arthur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Daniel_Huntington_-_Chester_Alan_Arthur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Daniel_Huntington_-_Chester_Alan_Arthur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3886" data-file-height="6254" /></a>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_Chester_A._Arthur" title="List of federal judges appointed by Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur judicial appointments</a></div>
<p>Arthur made appointments to fill two vacancies on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" title="United States Supreme Court" class="mw-redirect">United States Supreme Court</a>. The first vacancy arose in July 1881 with the death of <a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States">Associate Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Clifford" title="Nathan Clifford">Nathan Clifford</a>, a Democrat who had been a member of the Court since before the Civil War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195-174"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur nominated <a href="/wiki/Horace_Gray" title="Horace Gray">Horace Gray</a>, a distinguished jurist from the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Supreme_Judicial_Court" title="Massachusetts Supreme Judicial Court">Massachusetts Supreme Judicial Court</a> to replace him, and the nomination was easily confirmed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195-174"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> The second vacancy occurred when Associate Justice <a href="/wiki/Ward_Hunt" title="Ward Hunt">Ward Hunt</a> retired in January 1882. Arthur first nominated his old political boss, <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Conkling" title="Roscoe Conkling">Roscoe Conkling</a>; he doubted that Conkling would accept, but felt obligated to offer a high office to his former patron.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195_174-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195-174"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate confirmed the nomination but, as expected, Conkling declined it,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195_174-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195-174"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> the last time a confirmed nominee declined his appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-Senate.2C_SCOTUS_noms_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Senate.2C_SCOTUS_noms-175"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> Senator George Edmunds was Arthur's next choice, but he declined to be considered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoenecke76_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoenecke76-176"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> Instead, Arthur nominated <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Blatchford" title="Samuel Blatchford">Samuel Blatchford</a>, who had been a judge on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Second_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit">Second Circuit Court of Appeals</a> for the prior four years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195_174-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195-174"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> Blatchford accepted, and his nomination was approved within two weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195_174-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975260.E2.80.93261Howe195-174"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> Blatchford served on the Court until his death in 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-FJC.2C_Blatchford_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FJC.2C_Blatchford-177"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement.2C_death.2C_and_memorials">Retirement, death, and memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chester_A._Arthur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Retirement, death, and memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chet_Arthur_Mad_Sq_jeh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Bronze statue of a man in a city park" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Chet_Arthur_Mad_Sq_jeh.JPG/170px-Chet_Arthur_Mad_Sq_jeh.JPG" width="170" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Chet_Arthur_Mad_Sq_jeh.JPG/255px-Chet_Arthur_Mad_Sq_jeh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Chet_Arthur_Mad_Sq_jeh.JPG/340px-Chet_Arthur_Mad_Sq_jeh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3888" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chet_Arthur_Mad_Sq_jeh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chester A. Arthur statue at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square" title="Madison Square">Madison Square</a> in New York City, Bissell <i>1898</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Arthur left office in 1885 and returned to his New York City home. Two months before the end of his term, several New York Stalwarts approached him to request that he run for United States Senate, but he declined, preferring to return to his old law practice at Arthur, Knevals &amp; Ransom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975412.E2.80.93414_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975412.E2.80.93414-178"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> His health limited his activity with the firm, and Arthur served only <a href="/wiki/Of_counsel" title="Of counsel">of counsel</a>. He took on few assignments with the firm and was often too ill to leave his house.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418-179"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> He managed a few public appearances, until the end of 1885.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418-179"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After summering in <a href="/wiki/New_London,_Connecticut" title="New London, Connecticut">New London, Connecticut</a>, in 1886, he returned quite ill and, on November&#160;16, ordered nearly all of his papers, both personal and official, burned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418-179"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span>[</span>k<span>]</span></a></sup> The next morning, Arthur suffered a <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_hemorrhage" title="Cerebral hemorrhage">cerebral hemorrhage</a> and never regained consciousness; he died the following day at the age of 57.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418_179-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418-179"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> On November&#160;22, a private funeral was held at the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Heavenly_Rest" title="Church of the Heavenly Rest">Church of the Heavenly Rest</a> in New York City, attended by President Cleveland and ex-President Hayes, among other notables.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975418.E2.80.93419_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975418.E2.80.93419-182"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> Arthur was buried with his family members and ancestors in the <a href="/wiki/Albany_Rural_Cemetery" title="Albany Rural Cemetery">Albany Rural Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Menands,_New_York" title="Menands, New York">Menands, New York</a>. He was laid beside his wife in a <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> on a large corner of the plot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418_179-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975416.E2.80.93418-179"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1889, a monument was placed on Arthur's burial plot by sculptor <a href="/wiki/Ephraim_Keyser" title="Ephraim Keyser">Ephraim Keyser</a> of New York, consisting of a giant bronze female angel figure placing a bronze palm leaf on a granite sarcophagus.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_01_07_1894_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_01_07_1894-183"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1898, the Arthur memorial statue—a fifteen-foot, bronze figure of Arthur standing on a <a href="/wiki/Barre_Granite" title="Barre Granite">Barre Granite</a> pedestal—was created by sculptor <a href="/wiki/George_Edwin_Bissell" title="George Edwin Bissell">George Edwin Bissell</a> and installed at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square" title="Madison Square">Madison Square</a>, in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975419_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975419-184"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> The statue was dedicated in 1899 and unveiled by Arthur's sister, Mary Arthur McElroy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975419_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975419-184"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> At the dedication, Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/Elihu_Root" title="Elihu Root">Elihu Root</a> described Arthur as "wise in statesmanship and firm and effective in administration", while acknowledging that Arthur was isolated in office and unloved by his own party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975419_184-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975419-184"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Arthur's unpopularity in life carried over into his assessment by historians, and his reputation after leaving office disappeared.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell139_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell139-185"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1935, historian George F. Howe said that Arthur had achieved "an obscurity in strange contrast to his significant part in American history."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe288_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe288-186"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1975, however, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_C._Reeves" title="Thomas C. Reeves">Thomas C. Reeves</a> would write that Arthur's "appointments, if unspectacular, were unusually sound; the corruption and scandal that dominated business and politics of the period did not tarnish his administration."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves1975420_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves1975420-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> As 2004 biographer <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Karabell" title="Zachary Karabell">Zachary Karabell</a> wrote, although Arthur was "physically stretched and emotionally strained, he strove to do what was right for the country."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell139_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell139-185"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> Indeed, Howe had earlier surmised, "Arthur adopted [a code] for his own political behavior but subject to three restraints: he remained to everyone a man of his word; he kept scrupulously free from corrupt graft; he maintained a personal dignity, affable and genial though he might be. These restraints...distinguished him sharply from the stereotype politician."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe290_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe290-187"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
